<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Wave</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about waves in the scientific sense. For waves on the surface of the ocean or lakes, see <a href="/wiki/Wind_wave" title="Wind wave">Wind wave</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Wave_(disambiguation)" title="Wave (disambiguation)">Wave (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, a <b>wave</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Oscillation" title="Oscillation">oscillation</a> accompanied by a transfer of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>. <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">Frequency</a> refers to the addition of time. <i>Wave motion</i> transfers energy from one point to another, which displace <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Particles" title="Particles">particles</a> of the transmission medium–that is, with little or no associated <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> transport. Waves consist, instead, of <a href="/wiki/Oscillation" title="Oscillation">oscillations</a> or <a href="/wiki/Vibration" title="Vibration">vibrations</a> (of a physical quantity), around almost fixed locations.</p>
<p>A wave is a disturbance that transfers energy through matter or space. There are two main types of waves. <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_wave" title="Mechanical wave">Mechanical waves</a> propagate through a medium, and the substance of this medium is deformed. <a href="/wiki/Restoring_force" title="Restoring force">Restoring forces</a> then reverse the deformation. For example, <a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">sound</a> waves propagate via air molecules colliding with their neighbors. When the molecules collide, they also bounce away from each other (a restoring force). This keeps the molecules from continuing to travel in the direction of the wave.</p>
<p>The second main type, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave" title="Electromagnetic wave">electromagnetic waves</a>, do not require a medium. Instead, they consist of periodic oscillations of electrical and magnetic fields originally generated by charged particles, and can therefore travel through a <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a>. These types vary in <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a>, and include <a href="/wiki/Radio_wave" title="Radio wave">radio waves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwaves</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infrared_radiation" title="Infrared radiation">infrared radiation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible light</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation" title="Ultraviolet radiation">ultraviolet radiation</a>, <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>.</p>
<p>Waves are described by a <a href="/wiki/Wave_equation" title="Wave equation">wave equation</a> which sets out how the disturbance proceeds over time. The mathematical form of this equation varies depending on the type of wave. Further, the behavior of particles in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> are described by waves. In addition, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_radiation" title="Gravitational radiation">gravitational waves</a> also travel through space, which are a result of a vibration or movement in <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_field" title="Gravitational field">gravitational fields</a>.</p>
<p>A wave can be <a href="/wiki/Transverse_wave" title="Transverse wave">transverse</a>, where a disturbance creates oscillations that are perpendicular to the propagation of energy transfer, or <a href="/wiki/Longitudinal_wave" title="Longitudinal wave">longitudinal</a>: the oscillations are <a href="/wiki/Parallel_(geometry)" title="Parallel (geometry)">parallel</a> to the direction of energy propagation. While mechanical waves can be both transverse and longitudinal, all electromagnetic waves are transverse in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_space" title="Free space">free space</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="General_features">General features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: General features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1320" data-file-width="1920" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg/300px-2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg/450px-2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg/600px-2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2006-01-14_Surface_waves.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Surface_wave" title="Surface wave">Surface waves</a> in <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> showing water ripples</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A single, all-encompassing definition for the term <i>wave</i> is not straightforward. A <a href="/wiki/Vibration" title="Vibration">vibration</a> can be defined as a <i>back-and-forth</i> motion around a reference value. However, a vibration is not necessarily a wave. An attempt to define the necessary and sufficient characteristics that qualify a <a href="/wiki/Phenomenon" title="Phenomenon">phenomenon</a> as a <i>wave</i> results in a blurred line.</p>
<p>The term <i>wave</i> is often intuitively understood as referring to a transport of spatial disturbances that are generally not accompanied by a motion of the medium occupying this space as a whole. In a wave, the <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Vibration" title="Vibration">vibration</a> is moving away from the source in the form of a disturbance within the surrounding medium (<a href="#CITEREFHall1980">Hall 1980</a>, p. 8). However, this motion is problematic for a <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave" title="Standing wave">standing wave</a> (for example, a wave on a string), where <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> is moving in both directions equally, or for electromagnetic (e.g., light) waves in a <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a>, where the concept of medium does not apply and interaction with a target is the key to wave detection and practical applications. There are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_waves" title="Water waves">water waves</a> on the ocean surface; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_rays" title="Gamma rays">gamma waves</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_waves" title="Light waves">light waves</a> emitted by the Sun; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microwaves" title="Microwaves">microwaves</a> used in microwave ovens and in <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> equipment; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_waves" title="Radio waves">radio waves</a> broadcast by radio stations; and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sound_waves" title="Sound waves">sound waves</a> generated by radio receivers, telephone handsets and living creatures (as voices), to mention only a few wave phenomena.</p>
<p>It may appear that the description of waves is closely related to their physical origin for each specific instance of a wave process. For example, <a href="/wiki/Acoustics" title="Acoustics">acoustics</a> is distinguished from <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a> in that sound waves are related to a mechanical rather than an electromagnetic wave transfer caused by <a href="/wiki/Vibration" title="Vibration">vibration</a>. Concepts such as <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">inertia</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Elasticity_(physics)" title="Elasticity (physics)">elasticity</a>, become therefore crucial in describing acoustic (as distinct from optic) wave processes. This difference in origin introduces certain wave characteristics particular to the properties of the medium involved. For example, in the case of air: <a href="/wiki/Vortex" title="Vortex">vortices</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">radiation pressure</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shock_waves" title="Shock waves">shock waves</a> etc.; in the case of solids: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rayleigh_waves" title="Rayleigh waves">Rayleigh waves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(chemistry)" title="Dispersion (chemistry)">dispersion</a>; and so on....</p>
<p>Other properties, however, although usually described in terms of origin, may be generalized to all waves. For such reasons, wave theory represents a particular branch of <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> that is concerned with the properties of wave processes independently of their physical origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostrovsky_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ostrovsky-1">[1]</a></sup> For example, based on the mechanical origin of acoustic waves, a moving disturbance in space–time can exist if and only if the medium involved is neither infinitely stiff nor infinitely pliable. If all the parts making up a medium were rigidly <i>bound</i>, then they would all vibrate as one, with no delay in the transmission of the vibration and therefore no wave motion. On the other hand, if all the parts were independent, then there would not be any transmission of the vibration and again, no wave motion. Although the above statements are meaningless in the case of waves that do not require a medium, they reveal a characteristic that is relevant to all waves regardless of origin: within a wave, the <a href="/wiki/Phase_(waves)" title="Phase (waves)">phase</a> of a vibration (that is, its position within the vibration cycle) is different for adjacent points in space because the vibration reaches these points at different times.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematical_description_of_one-dimensional_waves">Mathematical description of one-dimensional waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Mathematical description of one-dimensional waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wave_equation">Wave equation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Wave equation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Wave_equation" title="Wave equation">Wave equation</a> and <a href="/wiki/D%27Alembert%27s_formula" title="D'Alembert's formula">D'Alembert's formula</a></div>
<p>Consider a traveling <a href="/wiki/Transverse_wave" title="Transverse wave">transverse wave</a> (which may be a <a href="/wiki/Pulse_(physics)" title="Pulse (physics)">pulse</a>) on a string (the medium). Consider the string to have a single spatial dimension. Consider this wave as traveling</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="287" data-file-width="512" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg/200px-Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg/300px-Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg/400px-Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nonsinusoidal_wavelength.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wavelength <i>λ</i>, can be measured between any two corresponding points on a waveform</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Superpositionprinciple.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Animation of two waves, the green wave moves to the right while blue wave moves to the left, the net red wave amplitude at each point is the sum of the amplitudes of the individual waves. Note that f(x,t) + g(x,t) = u(x,t)</div>
</div>

E.g., let the positive <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<li>with constant <a href="/wiki/Amplitude" title="Amplitude">amplitude</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</ul>
</li>
<li>with constant <a href="/wiki/Waveform" title="Waveform">waveform</a>, or shape</li>
</ul>

<p>General solutions are based upon <a href="/wiki/Duhamel%27s_principle" title="Duhamel's principle">Duhamel's principle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Struwe_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Struwe-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wave_forms">Wave forms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Wave forms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Waveform" title="Waveform">Waveform</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waveforms.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="672" data-file-width="780" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Waveforms.svg/280px-Waveforms.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Waveforms.svg/420px-Waveforms.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Waveforms.svg/560px-Waveforms.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waveforms.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Sine_wave" title="Sine wave">Sine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Square_wave" title="Square wave">square</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triangle_wave" title="Triangle wave">triangle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sawtooth_wave" title="Sawtooth wave">sawtooth</a> waveforms.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The form or shape of <i>F</i> in <a href="/wiki/D%27Alembert%27s_formula" title="D'Alembert's formula">d'Alembert's formula</a> involves the argument <i>x − vt</i>. Constant values of this argument correspond to constant values of <i>F</i>, and these constant values occur if <i>x</i> increases at the same rate that <i>vt</i> increases. That is, the wave shaped like the function <i>F</i> will move in the positive <i>x</i>-direction at velocity <i>v</i> (and <i>G</i> will propagate at the same speed in the negative <i>x</i>-direction).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lyons_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lyons-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the case of a periodic function <i>F</i> with period <i>λ</i>, that is, <i>F</i>(<i>x + λ</i> − <i>vt</i>) = <i>F</i>(<i>x</i> − <i>vt</i>), the periodicity of <i>F</i> in space means that a snapshot of the wave at a given time <i>t</i> finds the wave varying periodically in space with period <i>λ</i> (the <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> of the wave). In a similar fashion, this periodicity of <i>F</i> implies a periodicity in time as well: <i>F</i>(<i>x</i> − <i>v(t + T)</i>) = <i>F</i>(<i>x</i> − <i>vt</i>) provided <i>vT</i> = <i>λ</i>, so an observation of the wave at a fixed location <i>x</i> finds the wave undulating periodically in time with period <i>T = λ</i>/<i>v</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McPherson0_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-McPherson0-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amplitude_and_modulation">Amplitude and modulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Amplitude and modulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amplitudemodulation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="854" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Amplitudemodulation.gif/220px-Amplitudemodulation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Amplitudemodulation.gif/330px-Amplitudemodulation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Amplitudemodulation.gif/440px-Amplitudemodulation.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amplitudemodulation.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Amplitude modulation can be achieved through f(x,t) = 1.00*sin(2*pi/0.10*(x-1.00*t)) and g(x,t) = 1.00*sin(2*pi/0.11*(x-1.00*t))only the resultant is visible to improve clarity of waveform.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wave_packet.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3964" data-file-width="5478" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Wave_packet.svg/220px-Wave_packet.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Wave_packet.svg/330px-Wave_packet.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Wave_packet.svg/440px-Wave_packet.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wave_packet.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Illustration of the <i>envelope</i> (the slowly varying red curve) of an amplitude-modulated wave. The fast varying blue curve is the <i>carrier</i> wave, which is being modulated.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Amplitude_modulation" title="Amplitude modulation">Amplitude modulation</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Frequency_modulation" title="Frequency modulation">Frequency modulation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phase_modulation" title="Phase modulation">Phase modulation</a></div>
<p>The amplitude of a wave may be constant (in which case the wave is a <i>c.w.</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Continuous_wave" title="Continuous wave">continuous wave</a></i>), or may be <i>modulated</i> so as to vary with time and/or position. The outline of the variation in amplitude is called the <i>envelope</i> of the wave. Mathematically, the <a href="/wiki/Amplitude_modulation" title="Amplitude modulation">modulated wave</a> can be written in the form:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jirauschek_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jirauschek-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kneub.C3.BChl_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kneub.C3.BChl-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lundstrom_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lundstrom-10">[10]</a></sup></p>

<p>showing that the envelope moves with the group velocity and retains its shape. Otherwise, in cases where the group velocity varies with wavelength, the pulse shape changes in a manner often described using an <i>envelope equation</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chen_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chen-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Recami_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Recami-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phase_velocity_and_group_velocity">Phase velocity and group velocity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Phase velocity and group velocity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Phase_velocity" title="Phase velocity">Phase velocity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Group_velocity" title="Group velocity">Group velocity</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Envelope_(waves)#Phase_and_group_velocity" title="Envelope (waves)">Envelope (waves) § Phase and group velocity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wave_group.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="33" data-file-width="466" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Wave_group.gif/220px-Wave_group.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Wave_group.gif/330px-Wave_group.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Wave_group.gif/440px-Wave_group.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wave_group.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(water_waves)" title="Dispersion (water waves)">Frequency dispersion</a> in groups of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravity_waves" title="Gravity waves">gravity waves</a> on the surface of deep water. The red dot moves with the <a href="/wiki/Phase_velocity" title="Phase velocity">phase velocity</a>, and the green dots propagate with the <a href="/wiki/Group_velocity" title="Group velocity">group velocity</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are two velocities that are associated with waves, the <a href="/wiki/Phase_velocity" title="Phase velocity">phase velocity</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Group_velocity" title="Group velocity">group velocity</a>. To understand them, one must consider several types of waveform. For simplification, examination is restricted to one dimension.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wave_opposite-group-phase-velocity.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="154" data-file-width="250" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Wave_opposite-group-phase-velocity.gif/220px-Wave_opposite-group-phase-velocity.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c7/Wave_opposite-group-phase-velocity.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wave_opposite-group-phase-velocity.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This shows a wave with the Group velocity and Phase velocity going in different directions.</div>
</div>
</div>

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle A_{o}(k_{1})=N\ e^{-\sigma ^{2}(k_{1}-k)^{2}/2}\ ,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="A_{o}(k_{1})=N\ e^{-\sigma ^{2}(k_{1}-k)^{2}/2}\ ," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/73186856799e004e94134266f2881621d38b4a6f" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:25.945ex; height:3.676ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where σ determines the spread of <i>k</i><sub>1</sub>-values about <i>k</i>, and <i>N</i> is the amplitude of the wave.</p>
<p>The exponential function inside the integral for ψ oscillates rapidly with its argument, say φ(<i>k</i><sub>1</sub>), and where it varies rapidly, the exponentials cancel each other out, <a href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interfere</a> destructively, contributing little to ψ.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Messiah_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Messiah-13">[13]</a></sup> However, an exception occurs at the location where the argument φ of the exponential varies slowly. (This observation is the basis for the method of <a href="/wiki/Stationary_phase_approximation" title="Stationary phase approximation">stationary phase</a> for evaluation of such integrals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Orland_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Orland-15">[15]</a></sup>) The condition for φ to vary slowly is that its rate of change with <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> be small; this rate of variation is:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Messiah_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Messiah-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \left.{\frac {d\varphi }{dk_{1}}}\right|_{k_{1}=k}=x-t\left.{\frac {d\omega }{dk_{1}}}\right|_{k_{1}=k}+\left.{\frac {d\alpha }{dk_{1}}}\right|_{k_{1}=k}\ ,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\left.{\frac {d\varphi }{dk_{1}}}\right|_{k_{1}=k}=x-t\left.{\frac {d\omega }{dk_{1}}}\right|_{k_{1}=k}+\left.{\frac {d\alpha }{dk_{1}}}\right|_{k_{1}=k}\ ," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0aff539c04854f2285c8158d4b004315f01785f4" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:39.498ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where the evaluation is made at <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> = <i>k</i> because <i>A(k</i><sub>1</sub><i>)</i> is centered there. This result shows that the position <i>x</i> where the phase changes slowly, the position where ψ is appreciable, moves with time at a speed called the <i>group velocity</i>:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>g</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi>d</mi>
<mi>ω<!-- ω --></mi>
</mrow>
<mrow>
<mi>d</mi>
<mi>k</mi>
</mrow>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mtext> </mtext>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v_{g}={\frac {d\omega }{dk}}\ .}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="v_{g}={\frac {d\omega }{dk}}\ ." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ac6040244de8b4bbf93116fe8c868b60865ce88e" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:10.032ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The group velocity therefore depends upon the <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_relation" title="Dispersion relation">dispersion relation</a> connecting ω and <i>k</i>. For example, in quantum mechanics the energy of a particle represented as a wave packet is <i>E</i> = ħω = (ħ<i>k</i>)<sup>2</sup>/(2<i>m</i>). Consequently, for that wave situation, the group velocity is</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>v</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>g</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mi class="MJX-variant">ℏ<!-- ℏ --></mi>
<mi>k</mi>
</mrow>
<mi>m</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mtext> </mtext>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v_{g}={\frac {\hbar k}{m}}\ ,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="v_{g}={\frac {\hbar k}{m}}\ ," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cc652f8614c5190e2c233b33105f4df1c30b2ab4" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:9.888ex; height:5.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>showing that the velocity of a localized particle in quantum mechanics is its group velocity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Messiah_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-Messiah-13">[13]</a></sup> Because the group velocity varies with <i>k</i>, the shape of the wave packet broadens with time, and the particle becomes less localized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fitt_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fitt-16">[16]</a></sup> In other words, the velocity of the constituent waves of the wave packet travel at a rate that varies with their wavelength, so some move faster than others, and they cannot maintain the same <a href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interference pattern</a> as the wave propagates.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sinusoidal_waves">Sinusoidal waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Sinusoidal waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinusoidal_wave" title="Sinusoidal wave">Sinusoidal wave</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-style" role="presentation">

<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/20px-Edit-clear.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/30px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></td>

</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Simple_harmonic_motion_animation.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="242" data-file-width="512" height="104" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Simple_harmonic_motion_animation.gif/220px-Simple_harmonic_motion_animation.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Simple_harmonic_motion_animation.gif/330px-Simple_harmonic_motion_animation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Simple_harmonic_motion_animation.gif/440px-Simple_harmonic_motion_animation.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>

Sinusoidal waves correspond to <a href="/wiki/Simple_harmonic_motion" title="Simple harmonic motion">simple harmonic motion</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mathematically, the most basic wave is the (spatially) one-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Sine_wave" title="Sine wave">sine wave</a> (or <i>harmonic wave</i> or <i>sinusoid</i>) with an amplitude <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>u</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle u}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="u" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3e6bb763d22c20916ed4f0bb6bd49d7470cffd8" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.34ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> described by the equation:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>u</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>A</mi>
<mi>sin</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>k</mi>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>−<!-- − --></mo>
<mi>ω<!-- ω --></mi>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo>+</mo>
<mi>ϕ<!-- ϕ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mtext> </mtext>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle u(x,t)=A\sin(kx-\omega t+\phi )\ ,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="u(x,t)=A\sin(kx-\omega t+\phi )\ ," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a444e5ec7d65ac2fe27502c571e2a2e7e6a7f564" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:29.575ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where</p>
<ul>
<li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

<mi>A</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle A}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="A" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7daff47fa58cdfd29dc333def748ff5fa4c923e3" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.754ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> is the maximum <a href="/wiki/Amplitude" title="Amplitude">amplitude</a> of the wave, maximum distance from the highest point of the disturbance in the medium (the crest) to the equilibrium point during one wave cycle. In the illustration to the right, this is the maximum vertical distance between the baseline and the wave.</li>
<li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>x</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.34ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is the space <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coordinate" title="Coordinate">coordinate</a></li>
<li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</ul>
<p>The units of the amplitude depend on the type of wave. Transverse mechanical waves (e.g., a wave on a string) have an amplitude expressed as a <a href="/wiki/Distance" title="Distance">distance</a> (e.g., meters), longitudinal mechanical waves (e.g., sound waves) use units of pressure (e.g., pascals), and electromagnetic waves (a form of transverse vacuum wave) express the amplitude in terms of its <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a> (e.g., volts/meter).</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lambda }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\lambda " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b43d0ea3c9c025af1be9128e62a18fa74bedda2a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.366ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the distance between two sequential crests or troughs (or other equivalent points), generally is measured in meters. A <a href="/wiki/Wavenumber" title="Wavenumber">wavenumber</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>k</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle k}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="k" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3c9a2c7b599b37105512c5d570edc034056dd40" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.222ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, the spatial frequency of the wave in <a href="/wiki/Radian" title="Radian">radians</a> per unit distance (typically per meter), can be associated with the wavelength by the relation</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>k</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
</mrow>
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle k={\frac {2\pi }{\lambda }}.\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="k={\frac {2\pi }{\lambda }}.\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e8b858ae49fc5c3beb98faac0b0d361befc3b35" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:8.726ex; height:5.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Period_(physics)" title="Period (physics)">period</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>T</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle T}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="T" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ec7200acd984a1d3a3d7dc455e262fbe54f7f6e0" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.647ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the time for one complete cycle of an oscillation of a wave. The <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>f</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="f" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/132e57acb643253e7810ee9702d9581f159a1c61" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.289ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is the number of periods per unit time (per second) and is typically measured in <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">hertz</a> denoted as Hz. These are related by:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>f</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mn>1</mn>
<mi>T</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f={\frac {1}{T}}.\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="f={\frac {1}{T}}.\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/89533dc4b7b1a4156843ec72dac86410fd4fa014" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:7.925ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>In other words, the frequency and period of a wave are reciprocals.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Angular_frequency" title="Angular frequency">angular frequency</a> <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>ω<!-- ω --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \omega }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\omega " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/48eff443f9de7a985bb94ca3bde20813ea737be8" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.456ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> represents the frequency in radians per second. It is related to the frequency or period by</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>ω<!-- ω --></mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
<mi>f</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
</mrow>
<mi>T</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>.</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \omega =2\pi f={\frac {2\pi }{T}}.\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\omega =2\pi f={\frac {2\pi }{T}}.\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b5f2414f5214a43ea0cf761ba8603b6dee6ed374" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:15.874ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>The wavelength <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lambda }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\lambda " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b43d0ea3c9c025af1be9128e62a18fa74bedda2a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.366ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> of a sinusoidal waveform traveling at constant speed <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>v</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="v" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e07b00e7fc0847fbd16391c778d65bc25c452597" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.138ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is given by:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cassidy_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cassidy-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>v</mi>
<mi>f</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lambda ={\frac {v}{f}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\lambda ={\frac {v}{f}}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d5d42465a51464c789a2224d687dd5517b5d9013" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:7.257ex; height:5.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>v</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="v" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e07b00e7fc0847fbd16391c778d65bc25c452597" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.138ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> is called the phase speed (magnitude of the <a href="/wiki/Phase_velocity" title="Phase velocity">phase velocity</a>) of the wave and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>f</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle f}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="f" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/132e57acb643253e7810ee9702d9581f159a1c61" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.289ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is the wave's frequency.</p>
<p>Wavelength can be a useful concept even if the wave is not <a href="/wiki/Periodic_function" title="Periodic function">periodic</a> in space. For example, in an ocean wave approaching shore, the incoming wave undulates with a varying <i>local</i> wavelength that depends in part on the depth of the sea floor compared to the wave height. The analysis of the wave can be based upon comparison of the local wavelength with the local water depth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pinet2_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pinet2-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although arbitrary wave shapes will propagate unchanged in lossless <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear_time-invariant_system" title="Linear time-invariant system">linear time-invariant systems</a>, in the presence of dispersion the <a href="/wiki/Sine_wave" title="Sine wave">sine wave</a> is the unique shape that will propagate unchanged but for phase and amplitude, making it easy to analyze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kramers%E2%80%93Kronig_relation" title="Kramers–Kronig relation">Kramers–Kronig relations</a>, a linear medium with dispersion also exhibits loss, so the sine wave propagating in a dispersive medium is attenuated in certain frequency ranges that depend upon the medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tielens_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tielens-20">[20]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sine_function" title="Sine function">sine function</a> is periodic, so the <a href="/wiki/Sine_wave" title="Sine wave">sine wave</a> or sinusoid has a <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelength</a> in space and a period in time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Filippov_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Filippov-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stein1_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stein1-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The sinusoid is defined for all times and distances, whereas in physical situations we usually deal with waves that exist for a limited span in space and duration in time. Fortunately, an arbitrary wave shape can be decomposed into an infinite set of sinusoidal waves by the use of <a href="/wiki/Fourier_analysis" title="Fourier analysis">Fourier analysis</a>. As a result, the simple case of a single sinusoidal wave can be applied to more general cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stein2_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stein2-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwinger_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwinger-24">[24]</a></sup> In particular, many media are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear" title="Linear">linear</a>, or nearly so, so the calculation of arbitrary wave behavior can be found by adding up responses to individual sinusoidal waves using the <a href="/wiki/Superposition_principle" title="Superposition principle">superposition principle</a> to find the solution for a general waveform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewett_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jewett-25">[25]</a></sup> When a medium is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonlinear" title="Nonlinear">nonlinear</a>, the response to complex waves cannot be determined from a sine-wave decomposition.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plane_waves">Plane waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Plane waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plane_wave" title="Plane wave">Plane wave</a></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Standing_waves">Standing waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Standing waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave" title="Standing wave">Standing wave</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_resonance" title="Acoustic resonance">Acoustic resonance</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helmholtz_resonator" title="Helmholtz resonator">Helmholtz resonator</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Organ_pipe" title="Organ pipe">Organ pipe</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Standing_wave.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="750" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Standing_wave.gif/300px-Standing_wave.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Standing_wave.gif/450px-Standing_wave.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Standing_wave.gif/600px-Standing_wave.gif 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Standing_wave.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Standing wave in stationary medium. The red dots represent the wave <a href="/wiki/Node_(physics)" title="Node (physics)">nodes</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A standing wave, also known as a <i>stationary wave</i>, is a wave that remains in a constant position. This phenomenon can occur because the medium is moving in the opposite direction to the wave, or it can arise in a stationary medium as a result of <a href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interference</a> between two waves traveling in opposite directions.</p>
<p>The <i>sum</i> of two counter-propagating waves (of equal amplitude and frequency) creates a <i>standing wave</i>. Standing waves commonly arise when a boundary blocks further propagation of the wave, thus causing wave reflection, and therefore introducing a counter-propagating wave. For example, when a <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a> string is displaced, transverse waves propagate out to where the string is held in place at the <a href="/wiki/Bridge_(instrument)" title="Bridge (instrument)">bridge</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nut_(string_instrument)" title="Nut (string instrument)">nut</a>, where the waves are reflected back. At the bridge and nut, the two opposed waves are in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiphase" title="Antiphase">antiphase</a> and cancel each other, producing a <a href="/wiki/Node_(physics)" title="Node (physics)">node</a>. Halfway between two nodes there is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antinode" title="Antinode">antinode</a>, where the two counter-propagating waves <i>enhance</i> each other maximally. There is no net <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_transfer" title="Energy transfer">propagation of energy</a> over time.</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
<div style="margin:18.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Standing_waves_on_a_string.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="339" data-file-width="360" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Standing_waves_on_a_string.gif/120px-Standing_waves_on_a_string.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Standing_waves_on_a_string.gif/180px-Standing_waves_on_a_string.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Standing_waves_on_a_string.gif/240px-Standing_waves_on_a_string.gif 2x" width="120"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>One-dimensional standing waves; the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_frequency" title="Fundamental frequency">fundamental</a> mode and the first 5 <a href="/wiki/Overtone" title="Overtone">overtones</a>.</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
<div style="margin:43.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Drum_vibration_mode01.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="130" data-file-width="248" height="63" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Drum_vibration_mode01.gif/120px-Drum_vibration_mode01.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Drum_vibration_mode01.gif/180px-Drum_vibration_mode01.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Drum_vibration_mode01.gif/240px-Drum_vibration_mode01.gif 2x" width="120"/></a></div>

</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">

</div>

</div>

<p><br/></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2818" data-file-width="2309" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg/170px-Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg/255px-Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg/340px-Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Light_dispersion_of_a_mercury-vapor_lamp_with_a_flint_glass_prism_IPNr%C2%B00125.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Light beam exhibiting reflection, refraction, transmission and dispersion when encountering a prism</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Waves exhibit common behaviors under a number of standard situations, e. g.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission_and_media">Transmission and media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Transmission and media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Rectilinear_propagation" title="Rectilinear propagation">Rectilinear propagation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transmittance" title="Transmittance">Transmittance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Transmission_medium" title="Transmission medium">Transmission medium</a></div>
<p>Waves normally move in a straight line (i.e. rectilinearly) through a <i><a href="/wiki/Transmission_medium" title="Transmission medium">transmission medium</a></i>. Such media can be classified into one or more of the following categories:</p>
<ul>
<li>A <i>bounded medium</i> if it is finite in extent, otherwise an <i>unbounded medium</i></li>
<li>A <i>linear medium</i> if the amplitudes of different waves at any particular point in the medium can be added</li>
<li>A <i>uniform medium</i> or <i>homogeneous medium</i> if its physical properties are unchanged at different locations in space</li>
<li>An <i>anisotropic medium</i> if one or more of its physical properties differ in one or more directions</li>
<li>An <i>isotropic medium</i> if its physical properties are the <i>same</i> in all directions</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Absorption">Absorption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Absorption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(acoustics)" title="Absorption (acoustics)">Absorption (acoustics)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(electromagnetic_radiation)" title="Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)">Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)</a></div>
<p>Absorption of waves means, if a kind of wave strikes a matter, it will be absorbed by the matter. When a wave with that same natural frequency impinges upon an atom, then the electrons of that atom will be set into vibrational motion. If a wave of a given frequency strikes a material with electrons having the same vibrational frequencies, then those electrons will absorb the energy of the wave and transform it into vibrational motion.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reflection">Reflection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Reflection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Reflection_(physics)" title="Reflection (physics)">Reflection (physics)</a></div>
<p>When a wave strikes a reflective surface, it changes direction, such that the angle made by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incident_ray" title="Incident ray">incident wave</a> and line <a href="/wiki/Perpendicular" title="Perpendicular">normal</a> to the surface equals the angle made by the reflected wave and the same normal line.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interference">Interference</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Interference">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">Interference (wave propagation)</a></div>
<p>Waves that encounter each other combine through <a href="/wiki/Superposition_principle" title="Superposition principle">superposition</a> to create a new wave called an <a href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">interference pattern</a>. Important interference patterns occur for waves that are in <a href="/wiki/Phase_(waves)" title="Phase (waves)">phase</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Refraction">Refraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Refraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">Refraction</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wave_refraction.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="141" data-file-width="201" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Wave_refraction.gif/200px-Wave_refraction.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/Wave_refraction.gif 1.5x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wave_refraction.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sinusoidal traveling plane wave entering a region of lower wave velocity at an angle, illustrating the decrease in wavelength and change of direction (refraction) that results.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Refraction is the phenomenon of a wave changing its speed. Mathematically, this means that the size of the <a href="/wiki/Phase_velocity" title="Phase velocity">phase velocity</a> changes. Typically, refraction occurs when a wave passes from one <a href="/wiki/Transmission_medium" title="Transmission medium">medium</a> into another. The amount by which a wave is refracted by a material is given by the <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> of the material. The directions of incidence and refraction are related to the refractive indices of the two materials by <a href="/wiki/Snell%27s_law" title="Snell's law">Snell's law</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diffraction">Diffraction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Diffraction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">Diffraction</a></div>
<p>A wave exhibits diffraction when it encounters an obstacle that bends the wave or when it spreads after emerging from an opening. Diffraction effects are more pronounced when the size of the obstacle or opening is comparable to the wavelength of the wave.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polarization">Polarization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Polarization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">Polarization (waves)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg"><img alt="Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light Circular.Polarizer Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="348" data-file-width="791" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg/220px-Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg/330px-Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg/440px-Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Circular.Polarization.Circularly.Polarized.Light_Circular.Polarizer_Creating.Left.Handed.Helix.View.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The phenomenon of polarization arises when wave motion can occur simultaneously in two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthogonal" title="Orthogonal">orthogonal</a> directions. <a href="/wiki/Transverse_wave" title="Transverse wave">Transverse waves</a> can be polarized, for instance. When polarization is used as a descriptor without qualification, it usually refers to the special, simple case of <a href="/wiki/Linear_polarization" title="Linear polarization">linear polarization</a>. A transverse wave is linearly polarized if it oscillates in only one direction or plane. In the case of linear polarization, it is often useful to add the relative orientation of that plane, perpendicular to the direction of travel, in which the oscillation occurs, such as "horizontal" for instance, if the plane of polarization is parallel to the ground. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_waves" title="Electromagnetic waves">Electromagnetic waves</a> propagating in free space, for instance, are transverse; they can be polarized by the use of a <a href="/wiki/Polarizer" title="Polarizer">polarizing filter</a>.</p>
<p>Longitudinal waves, such as sound waves, do not exhibit polarization. For these waves there is only one direction of oscillation, that is, along the direction of travel.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dispersion">Dispersion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Dispersion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif/270px-Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif/405px-Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif/540px-Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif 2x" width="270"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Light_dispersion_conceptual_waves.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Schematic of light being dispersed by a prism. Click to see animation.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_relation" title="Dispersion relation">Dispersion relation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(optics)" title="Dispersion (optics)">Dispersion (optics)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(water_waves)" title="Dispersion (water waves)">Dispersion (water waves)</a></div>
<p>A wave undergoes dispersion when either the <a href="/wiki/Phase_velocity" title="Phase velocity">phase velocity</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Group_velocity" title="Group velocity">group velocity</a> depends on the wave frequency. Dispersion is most easily seen by letting white light pass through a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prism_(optics)" title="Prism (optics)">prism</a>, the result of which is to produce the spectrum of colours of the rainbow. <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> performed experiments with light and prisms, presenting his findings in the <i><a href="/wiki/Opticks" title="Opticks">Opticks</a></i> (1704) that white light consists of several colours and that these colours cannot be decomposed any further.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newton_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Newton-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical_waves">Mechanical waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Mechanical waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_wave" title="Mechanical wave">Mechanical wave</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Waves_on_strings">Waves on strings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Waves on strings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vibrating_string" title="Vibrating string">Vibrating string</a></div>
<p>The speed of a transverse wave traveling along a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vibrating_string" title="Vibrating string">vibrating string</a> ( <i>v</i> ) is directly proportional to the square root of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tension_(mechanics)" title="Tension (mechanics)">tension</a> of the string ( <i>T</i> ) over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linear_mass_density" title="Linear mass density">linear mass density</a> ( <i>μ</i> ):</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>v</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<msqrt>
<mfrac>
<mi>T</mi>
<mi>μ<!-- μ --></mi>
</mfrac>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v={\sqrt {\frac {T}{\mu }}},\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="v={\sqrt {\frac {T}{\mu }}},\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/041158596d625acab527f0fa38acb4d055fd96a3" style="vertical-align: -3.338ex; width:10.108ex; height:7.676ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where the linear density <i>μ</i> is the mass per unit length of the string.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acoustic_waves">Acoustic waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Acoustic waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Acoustic or <a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">sound</a> waves travel at speed given by</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mn>0</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mfrac>
</msqrt>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
<mspace width="thinmathspace"></mspace>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle v={\sqrt {\frac {B}{\rho _{0}}}},\,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="v={\sqrt {\frac {B}{\rho _{0}}}},\," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bae3d641cafcab33380b11e3834b52e861addcf3" style="vertical-align: -3.338ex; width:10.736ex; height:7.676ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>or the square root of the adiabatic bulk modulus divided by the ambient fluid density (see <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">speed of sound</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_waves">Water waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Water waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shallow_water_wave.gif"><img alt="Shallow water wave.gif" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="70" data-file-width="466" height="60" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Shallow_water_wave.gif/400px-Shallow_water_wave.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/be/Shallow_water_wave.gif 1.5x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shallow_water_wave.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_waves" title="Water waves">Water waves</a></div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ripple_tank" title="Ripple tank">Ripples</a> on the surface of a pond are actually a combination of transverse and longitudinal waves; therefore, the points on the surface follow orbital paths.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">Sound</a>—a mechanical wave that propagates through gases, liquids, solids and plasmas;</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inertial_waves" title="Inertial waves">Inertial waves</a>, which occur in rotating fluids and are restored by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a>;</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocean_surface_wave" title="Ocean surface wave">Ocean surface waves</a>, which are perturbations that propagate through water.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seismic_waves">Seismic waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Seismic waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seismic_waves" title="Seismic waves">Seismic waves</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shock_waves">Shock waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Shock waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="floatright"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Transonico-en.svg" title="Formation of a shock wave by a plane."><img alt="Formation of a shock wave by a plane." data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2504" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Transonico-en.svg/300px-Transonico-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Transonico-en.svg/450px-Transonico-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Transonico-en.svg/600px-Transonico-en.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
