<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Waves of <a href="/wiki/Traffic_wave" title="Traffic wave">traffic</a>, that is, propagation of different densities of motor vehicles, and so forth, which can be modeled as kinematic waves<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lighthill_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lighthill-27">[27]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Metachronal_rhythm" title="Metachronal rhythm">Metachronal wave</a> refers to the appearance of a traveling wave produced by coordinated sequential actions.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Electromagnetic_waves">Electromagnetic waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Electromagnetic waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png"><img alt="Onde electromagnétique.png" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1073" data-file-width="1621" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png/300px-Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png/450px-Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png/600px-Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Onde_electromagn%C3%A9tique.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">Electromagnetic radiation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">Electromagnetic spectrum</a></div>
<p>An electromagnetic wave consists of two waves that are oscillations of the <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic</a> fields. An electromagnetic wave travels in a direction that is at right angles to the oscillation direction of both fields. In the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> showed that, in <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a>, the electric and magnetic fields satisfy the <a href="/wiki/Wave_equation" title="Wave equation">wave equation</a> both with speed equal to that of the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>. From this emerged the idea that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">light</a> is an electromagnetic wave. Electromagnetic waves can have different frequencies (and thus wavelengths), giving rise to various types of radiation such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_waves" title="Radio waves">radio waves</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microwaves" title="Microwaves">microwaves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a>, visible light, <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-rays" title="X-rays">X-rays</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_rays" title="Gamma rays">Gamma rays</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_mechanical_waves">Quantum mechanical waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Quantum mechanical waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schrödinger equation">Schrödinger equation</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Wave_function" title="Wave function">Wave function</a></div>
<h3><span id="Schrödinger_equation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Schr.C3.B6dinger_equation">Schrödinger equation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Schrödinger equation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schrödinger equation">Schrödinger equation</a> describes the wave-like behavior of particles in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>. Solutions of this equation are <a href="/wiki/Wave_function" title="Wave function">wave functions</a> which can be used to describe the probability density of a particle.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dirac_equation">Dirac equation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Dirac equation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dirac_equation" title="Dirac equation">Dirac equation</a> is a relativistic wave equation detailing electromagnetic interactions. Dirac waves accounted for the fine details of the hydrogen spectrum in a completely rigorous way. The wave equation also implied the existence of a new form of matter, antimatter, previously unsuspected and unobserved and which was experimentally confirmed. In the context of quantum field theory, the Dirac equation is reinterpreted to describe quantum fields corresponding to spin-½ particles.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wave_packet_(dispersion).gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="115" data-file-width="200" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b0/Wave_packet_%28dispersion%29.gif" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wave_packet_(dispersion).gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A propagating wave packet; in general, the <i>envelope</i> of the wave packet moves at a different speed than the constituent waves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fromhold_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fromhold-28">[28]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="de_Broglie_waves">de Broglie waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: de Broglie waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Wave_packet" title="Wave packet">Wave packet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matter_wave" title="Matter wave">Matter wave</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Louis_de_Broglie" title="Louis de Broglie">Louis de Broglie</a> postulated that all particles with <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> have a wavelength</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mi>h</mi>
<mi>p</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lambda ={\frac {h}{p}},}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\lambda ={\frac {h}{p}}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ea61dba4f570d474341b6f5ff22b0f26f52c1a87" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:7.317ex; height:5.843ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where <i>h</i> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planck%27s_constant" title="Planck's constant">Planck's constant</a>, and <i>p</i> is the magnitude of the <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> of the particle. This hypothesis was at the basis of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>. Nowadays, this wavelength is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Broglie_wavelength" title="De Broglie wavelength">de Broglie wavelength</a>. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Cathode_ray_tube" title="Cathode ray tube">CRT</a> display have a de Broglie wavelength of about 10<sup>−13</sup> m.</p>
<p>A wave representing such a particle traveling in the <i>k</i>-direction is expressed by the wave function as follows:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>ψ<!-- ψ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">r</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>,</mo>
<mtext> </mtext>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>A</mi>
<mtext> </mtext>
<msup>
<mi>e</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>i</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">k</mi>
<mo>⋅<!-- ⋅ --></mo>
<mi mathvariant="bold">r</mi>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</msup>
<mtext> </mtext>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \psi (\mathbf {r} ,\ t=0)=A\ e^{i\mathbf {k\cdot r} }\ ,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\psi (\mathbf {r} ,\ t=0)=A\ e^{i\mathbf {k\cdot r} }\ ," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/39ab398ed72948dace20d688efd2ec34215532a2" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:22.062ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>where the wavelength is determined by the <a href="/wiki/Wave_vector" title="Wave vector">wave vector</a> <b>k</b> as:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>λ<!-- λ --></mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mfrac>
<mrow>
<mn>2</mn>
<mi>π<!-- π --></mi>
</mrow>
<mi>k</mi>
</mfrac>
</mrow>
<mtext> </mtext>
<mo>,</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \lambda ={\frac {2\pi }{k}}\ ,}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\lambda ={\frac {2\pi }{k}}\ ," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7a4b4cc29e15de64c1d529eb7e0f5d6ad4088b35" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:9.064ex; height:5.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>and the momentum by:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">p</mi>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi class="MJX-variant">ℏ<!-- ℏ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi mathvariant="bold">k</mi>
</mrow>
<mtext> </mtext>
<mo>.</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \mathbf {p} =\hbar \mathbf {k} \ .}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\mathbf {p} =\hbar \mathbf {k} \ ." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cebcb91c4ec9cacef3049b5818b1aaa25b7b6dcc" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.581ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>However, a wave like this with definite wavelength is not localized in space, and so cannot represent a particle localized in space. To localize a particle, de Broglie proposed a superposition of different wavelengths ranging around a central value in a <a href="/wiki/Wave_packet" title="Wave packet">wave packet</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marton_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marton-29">[29]</a></sup> a waveform often used in <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> to describe the <a href="/wiki/Wave_function" title="Wave function">wave function</a> of a particle. In a wave packet, the wavelength of the particle is not precise, and the local wavelength deviates on either side of the main wavelength value.</p>
<p>In representing the wave function of a localized particle, the <a href="/wiki/Wave_packet" title="Wave packet">wave packet</a> is often taken to have a <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_function" title="Gaussian function">Gaussian shape</a> and is called a <i>Gaussian wave packet</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wavepacket_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-wavepacket-30">[30]</a></sup> Gaussian wave packets also are used to analyze water waves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mei_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mei-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>For example, a Gaussian wavefunction ψ might take the form:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bromley_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bromley-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>ψ<!-- ψ --></mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>,</mo>
<mtext> </mtext>
<mi>t</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mn>0</mn>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>A</mi>
<mtext> </mtext>
<mi>exp</mi>
<mo>⁡<!-- ⁡ --></mo>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\psi (x,\ t=0)=A\ \exp \left(-{\frac {x^{2}}{2\sigma ^{2}}}+ik_{0}x\right)\ ," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d5049d1cccf073c4a7392525a305b5f9dd4eb614" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:39.062ex; height:6.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>at some initial time <i>t</i> = 0, where the central wavelength is related to the central wave vector <i>k</i><sub>0</sub> as λ<sub>0</sub> = 2π / <i>k</i><sub>0</sub>. It is well known from the theory of <a href="/wiki/Fourier_analysis" title="Fourier analysis">Fourier analysis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brandt_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brandt-33">[33]</a></sup> or from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heisenberg_uncertainty_principle" title="Heisenberg uncertainty principle">Heisenberg uncertainty principle</a> (in the case of quantum mechanics) that a narrow range of wavelengths is necessary to produce a localized wave packet, and the more localized the envelope, the larger the spread in required wavelengths. The <a href="/wiki/Fourier_transform" title="Fourier transform">Fourier transform</a> of a Gaussian is itself a Gaussian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaussian_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaussian-34">[34]</a></sup> Given the Gaussian:</p>
<dl>
<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>The Gaussian in space therefore is made up of waves:</p>
<dl>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="f(x)={\frac {1}{\sqrt {2\pi }}}\int _{-\infty }^{\infty }\ {\tilde {f}}(k)e^{ikx}\ dk\ ;" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/332af52d71f41681f52fc3a31597a58d05ea045f" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:31.433ex; height:6.343ex;"/></span></dd>
</dl>
<p>that is, a number of waves of wavelengths λ such that <i>k</i>λ = 2 π.</p>
<p>The parameter σ decides the spatial spread of the Gaussian along the <i>x</i>-axis, while the Fourier transform shows a spread in <a href="/wiki/Wave_vector" title="Wave vector">wave vector</a> <i>k</i> determined by 1/σ. That is, the smaller the extent in space, the larger the extent in <i>k</i>, and hence in λ = 2π/<i>k</i>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravity_waves">Gravity waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Gravity waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravity_waves" title="Gravity waves">Gravity waves</a> are waves generated in a fluid medium or at the interface between two media when the force of gravity or buoyancy tries to restore equilibrium. A ripple on a pond is one example.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational_waves">Gravitational waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Gravitational waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">Gravitational wave</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_radiation" title="Gravitational radiation">Gravitational waves</a> also travel through space. The first observation of gravitational waves was announced on 11 February 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Gravitational waves are disturbances in the curvature of <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a>, predicted by Einstein's theory of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="WKB_method">WKB method</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: WKB method">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WKB_method" title="WKB method">WKB method</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Slowly_varying_envelope_approximation" title="Slowly varying envelope approximation">Slowly varying envelope approximation</a></div>
<p>In a nonuniform medium, in which the wavenumber <i>k</i> can depend on the location as well as the frequency, the phase term <i>kx</i> is typically replaced by the integral of <i>k</i>(<i>x</i>)<i>dx</i>, according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WKB_method" title="WKB method">WKB method</a>. Such nonuniform traveling waves are common in many physical problems, including the mechanics of the <a href="/wiki/Cochlea" title="Cochlea">cochlea</a> and waves on hanging ropes.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electromagnetic_waves_2">Electromagnetic waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Electromagnetic waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_fluids">In fluids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: In fluids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<li><a href="/wiki/Cnoidal_wave" title="Cnoidal wave">Cnoidal wave</a>, in fluid dynamics</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Edge_wave" title="Edge wave">Edge wave</a>, a surface gravity wave fixed by refraction against a rigid boundary</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Faraday_wave" title="Faraday wave">Faraday wave</a>, a type of wave in liquids</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gravity_wave" title="Gravity wave">Gravity wave</a>, in fluid dynamics</li>
<li>Sound wave, a wave of <a href="/wiki/Sound" title="Sound">sound</a> through a medium such as air or water</li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Internal_wave" title="Internal wave">Internal wave</a>, a wave within a fluid medium</li>
<li>Tidal wave, a scientifically incorrect name for a <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_specific_types_of_waves">Other specific types of waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wave&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Other specific types of waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
